Question No 11 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0644/96)

Subject: Use of criterion of fairness under MGP IV and failure by certain Member States to comply with MGP III

Withe a view to the forthcoming decision to reduce the fishing capacity of the Community fleet under MGP IV, will the Council take into account the failures to comply with MGP III? is it always to be the same Member States who reduce their fleets when others are failing to do so? Will a criterion of fairness be introduced, so that, under MGP IV, the largest reductions will have to be made by the Member States which have so far not reduced their fleets, thereby failing to comply with the current legislation? Will there be recognition and encouragement for the loyalty to the Community of those who have made the greatest efforts to reduce their fleets and have complied with their obligations in order to conserve fishery resources?

Answer

1. The proposal for a Council Decision concerning the objectives and detailed rules for restructuring the Community fisheries sector for the period from 1 January 1997 to 31 December 2002, aimed at achieving a balance on a sustainable basis between resources and their exploitation – which will be the basis of the future MAGPs IV (Multiannual Guidance Programmes) – is currently under consideration by the Council.

2. The Commission has confirmed that the future objectives for restructuring the Community fleet under the MAGPs IV will take the objectives of the MAGPs III as a starting‐point. This will enable the efforts accomplished in the past to be taken into account. According to the Commission's view, a Member State that has achieved fleet reductions going beyond its objectives under the MAGPs III will thus find the objectives under the next programme lightened in consequence. Such a Member State will have a ‚credit’ which will make these objectives easier to achieve.

3. In this context, the Council draws the attention of the honourable Member to the ‚Report from the Commission to the Council on the progress of the MAGPs III at the end of 1995’ and to the ‚Commission Communication relating to management guidelines for the 4th generation of MAGPs IV’.

4. When coming to a decision on this proposal, the Council will of course see to it that the principles of nondiscrimination and equitable treatment as between national fleets are fully upheld.

5. Before reaching any such decision, the Council will have the benefit of the opinion of Parliament, which, as the honourable Member is aware, has been consulted on the proposal.

Question No 12 by Jan Andersson (H−0645/96)

Subject: The fight against drugs

Drug problems are on the increase in Europe. The Irish presidency has therefore made a positive move in putting the fight against drugs high on its agenda. One of the problems involved, however, is that the EU Member States have different views about whether certain drugs should be legalized and what amounts individuals should be allowed for their own personal use. These different views are also affecting international drugs trafficking.

How does the Irish presidency intend to tackle this problem?

Answer

Following the conclusions of the Madrid and Florence Summits, work has begun on determining the contribution which the possible harmonization of legislation might make to reducing supply and demand.

In March this year, the Commission organized a workshop on this subject. At present, the Group of Experts on Drugs is considering the matter.

The intention is that this Group's guidelines and proceedings should be presented at the Summit to be held by the European Council in Dublin in December.

I should add that the Irish presidency has formulated a programme on drugs which envisages, among other things, the adoption of seven joint actions and two resolutions. I can tell you that the work associated with this programme is making good progress.

Question No 13 by Jörn Svensson (H−0650/96)

Subject: Participation in ERM before EMU

The Swedish Government maintains that it does not have to comply with the requirements under the Maastricht Treaty for two years' participation in the ERM (European Exchange Rate Mechanism) before possible entry into the EMU. In this it has been directly contradicted in the Swedish media by Jürgen Stark, undersecretary of State in the German Government, and by the Belgian economist and EMU expert Paul de Grauwe.

Which view does the Council espouse?

Answer

The Council is not aware of the opinions or statements referred to by the honourable Member of the Parliament on the question of the participation of Sweden in the ERM before the country's possible entry into EMU.

The relevant provisions of the Treaty nevertheless provide sufficient guidance for the Council, allowing it to take in due course the decisions referred to in Article 109j of the Treaty.

In this context, however, the Council recalls that the recently adopted broad economic guidelines for the Community and the Member States stress that all Member states must continue to treat their exchange rate policies as a matter of common interest within the framework of the European Monetary System.

Question No 14 by Alex Smith (H−0659/96)

Subject: Free trade area with South Africa

Given the serious impact the EU‐South Africa FTA will have on countries which form part of the Southern African Customs Union agreement, what steps does the Council propose to take to ensure that these governments are fully consulted?

What scope is there likely to be within the process of negotiations for ensuring greater asymmetry of product coverage under the proposed FTA, in order to ensure that the broadest backs (the EU) carry the greatest burden, in terms of the adjustment costs associated with the introduction of an EU‐SA FTA?

What initiatives does the Irish presidency intend to take to assist Lesotho in a context where the FTA could lead to the loss of up to 35 % of current Lesothan Government revenue?

Answer

The negotiations on the Free Trade Area (FTA) between the EU and South Africa are bilateral and it is therefore up to South Africa to consult its SACU partners. Except for South Africa, the four other countries Lesotho, Swaziland, Botswana and Namibia participating in the Southern African Customs Union (SACU) are all ACP States, which are being currently informed of the state of play in the negotiations by the Commission. If these countries wish to make their positions known to the EU they can do so through the appropriate mechanisms of the Lomé Convention. Article 12 of the Convention states clearly that if the ACP States so request, consultations shall be held promptly so that account can be taken of their concerns. The Community intends of course to comply fully with this provision.

Since the EU's complementary negotiating directives concerning trade were presented to South Africa on 25 March 1996, the Union has been waiting for a reaction from the South African side. Before this reaction is forthcoming negotiations in this field cannot start and it is therefore not possible for the Council to pronounce itself upon the possible asymmetry that can be obtained in the negotiations.

As just stated the negotiations on the trade aspects have not yet started, and it is therefore premature to assess what impact their outcome can have for other countries. The right of initiative resides with the Commission, but I can assure you that due account will be taken of any serious consequences the outcome of the negotiations may have for other members of the SACU.

***

Question No 15 by Yannos Kranidiotis (H−0660/96)

Subject: Statements by Tansu Çiller on new customs tariffs which Turkey intends to levy on imports

According to the Deputy Prime Minister of Turkey, Mrs Tansu Çiller, Turkey intends to impose new customs tariffs of 6 % on imports.

The Agreement on Customs Union signed by Turkey on 6 March 1995 provides, inter alia, that the contracting parties may not impose customs tariffs or taxes on bilateral trade and are obliged to implement new customs tariffs in their relations with third countries.

Turkey's decision to impose new tariffs is therefore contrary to the commitments undertaken by Turkey vis‐à‐vis the European Union on the implementation of the Agreement on Customs Union.

Will the Council say how it views these statements by the Turkish Government and whether it intends to raise the matter during the forthcoming meeting of the EU‐Turkey Association Council?

Answer

The Council has no knowledge of Turkey's introduction of new customs duties amounting to 6 %, which would indeed be contrary to the provisions of the Agreement on Customs Union which came into force on 31 December 1995.

On the other hand, at the end of July 1996 the Turkish authorities did introduce a 6 % tax on credit transactions. That tax applies to all credit transactions intended to finance the purchase either of imported goods or of national products. At first sight, therefore, it is not considered discriminatory, since it affects all credit transactions without distinction. It does, however, constitute a measure of indirect taxation on imports since it is also imposed on credit transactions intended to finance imports, and for that reason, prior consultations with the Turkish authorities should have taken place. The Council is monitoring the matter closely and is awaiting a definitive analysis by the Commission's services, of the extent to which that tax is compatible with Turkey's obligations under the Agreement on Customs Union. If necessary, the Council could raise the matter at the next meeting of the EU‐Turkey Association Council, to remind Turkey that it must comply with its obligations.

***

Question No 16 by John McCartin (H−0662/96)

Subject: EU participation in the ASEAN Regional Forum

Does the President‐in‐Office believe that we can continue with an effective policy of EU political cooperation if some Member States insist on being represented individually as well as by the EU, as is the case, for example with the ASEAN Regional Forum, where Britain and France have recently applied for individual membership?

Answer

The European Union has been a participant in the ASEAN Regional Forum (ARF) since its inception in 1994. The European Union is represented by the presidency and the Commission at the Annual ARF Ministerial Meeting and by the EU Troika at the Track I (Governmental) Inter‐Sessional Support Group on Confidence Building Measures, other Inter‐Sessional Meetings, and the ARF Senior Official's Meeting. The European Union is also represented by the EU Troïka at Track II (Non‐Governmental) Seminars.

In advance of all ARF activities which I have mentioned, there is provision through the Common Foreign and Security Policy for all EU Member States to contribute to all aspects of ARF participation by the European Union e.g. speeches, interventions on particular topics, proposals for Seminars etc. In this regard I wish to advise the honourable Member that France has undertaken the organisation of an ARF Track II Seminar on Preventive Diplomacy, which will be held in Paris on 7/8 November 1996.

The ARF operates on the basis of consensus and contributions by participants are invited on a voluntary basis.

There are at present 21 participants in the ARF. I am aware that France and the United Kingdom together with a number of other States would like to become participants in the ARF. At the Third ARF Meeting, which was held in Jakarta on 23 July 1996, Ministers agreed on a Paper on Criteria for Participation in the ARF which, inter alia, provides that ‚all questions regarding participation should be decided by consultations among all ARF participants’. This process of consultation in relation to possible future participants has still to be initiated by the current ARF Chairman, Malaysia. The Council has not yet taken a position on this matter.

Question No 17 by Gérard Caudron (H−0667/96)

Subject: Imports of textile products and economic development

The signing of the GATT agreements giving rise to the WTO (World Trade Organization) was intended to reduce customs barriers and promote international trade.

In the textile industry, as in others, it has exacerbated wage and social disparities between the developed and developing countries.

Relocations to developing countries because of their low wage and social costs have resulted in the closure of many textile businesses and the loss of millions of jobs in Europe, without at the same time helping the developing countries.

In the face of these relocations and competitive devaluations there is an urgent need to protect the European textile sector and encourage the development of the ACP countries by raising their standard of living.

Could the Council not consider the application of an economic and social development levy on imports of textile products from developing countries or those with low labour costs, this being paid back to the exporting countries and reallocated partly for development and partly to the importers to finance their social security schemes to make up for the loss of wage contributions as a result of the imports?

Answer

1. In general terms, the honourable Member's question relates to the problems of the link between the multilateral trading system and the internationally recognized working standards, a link which is the subject of lively international debate. The Council recalls that the Commission has just passed it a communication on this subject which will be examined shortly by the Council's competent authorities in connection with the preparation of the first WTO ministerial conference to be held in Singapore from 9 to 13 December 1996.

2. With regard to the specific question put by the honourable Member on the introduction of an economic and social development levy to be applied to textile imports, the Council observes that no such proposal has ever been put to it by the Commission. Consequently, it has never had cause to comment on such a possibility.

Question No 18 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0670/96)

Subject: Murderous attack on peaceful demonstrators in Cyprus

Following the signature of the Customs Union Agreement with Turkey, the latter has not only failed to take any steps towards changing its stance on the Cyprus question but, on the contrary, has precipitated a dangerous increase in tension and stepped up its offensive, as shown by the unwarrantable action taken on 11 August 1996 by the Turkish occupying forces against unarmed and peaceful demonstrators, resulting in one death and dozens of casualties, contrary to the Council's predictions.

Will the Council make representations to the Turkish Government and demand explanations for the action taken by the Turkish occupying forces in Cyprus against peaceful demonstrators?

Will it call for those responsible for the death of an unarmed demonstrator and the dozens of casualties to be identified and punished?

Will it take steps in support of the demands of the peaceful demonstration for ‚freedom of movement within Cyprus’?

Question No 19 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0679/96)

Subject: Violence by Turks in Cyprus

The Council of Ministers has given repeated assurances that it will take positive action within the framework of the EU's economic agreements with Turkey (Customs Union, etc.) to ensure the development of democracy, human rights and the rule of law in Turkey. Can the Council give an account of any such specific action it has taken in recent months? Could it perhaps assure us that it will ask Turkey to identify and bring a criminal prosecution against the Turkish citizens who, with the full protection of the Turkish military occupation forces and in full view of European television cameras, killed two unarmed demonstrators (the first, Tassos Isaak, was killed with sticks and hatchets, and the second, S. Solomos, was shot in cold blood) during an unarmed demonstration on the dividing line between free Cyprus and occupied Cyprus?

Joint Answer

I propose to reply jointly to the questions of Mr Alavanos and Mr Papayannakis concerning the public disorder and loss of human life which took place during a demonstration organized to protest against the partition of the island (of Cyprus).

The declaration by the presidency on behalf of the European Union on 16 August well demonstrates the Council's position by strongly deploring the recent violence in Cyprus and the disproportionate use of force by the security forces in the north of Cyprus in response to unauthorized entry into the buffer zone. It condemned the brutal killings of Tassos Isaac and Solomos Solomos and expressed deep concern about the injuries sustained by many others, including United Nations peacekeeping personnel. The Union called for restraint and calm in the wake of these tragic events. It urged both sides to avoid further confrontation and to cooperate fully with UNFICYP in its efforts to defuse tensions.

The recent events have again highlighted the urgent need to intensify efforts to promote a comprehensive political settlement in Cyprus, under the aegis of the United Nations. The Council reiterates its full support for the work of the Secretary‐General of the United Nations and his special representative in this regard.

It similarly condemns the killing of a Turkish Cypriot soldier and wounding of another on 8 September.

Question No 20 by Georges Berthu (H−0686/96)

Subject: Abolition of Article 235

Opinion 2/94 of the Court of Justice of 28 March 1996, on the possibility of Community accession to the European Convention on Human Rights, includes an interesting passing comment on Article 235 of the Treaty, to the effect that the article ‚cannot serve as a basis for widening the scope of Community powers’, nor could it be ‚used as a basis for the adoption of provisions whose effect would, in substance, be to amend the Treaty without following the procedure which it provides for that purpose’.

This perfectly justified position substantially reduces the article's interest to the Union's institutions. Maintaining it would, in the circumstances, be a continual source of ambiguity, temptation and dispute.

Does the presidency not consider that, in view of this encouraging development, it is increasingly desirable to re‐table the issue of abolition of Article 235 in the negotiations at the Intergovernmental Conference?

Answer

The question of the deletion or amendment of Article 235 formed part of the series of measures put forward for negotiation at the Intergovernmental Conference, both by the Planning Group responsible for preparing the Conference and at the negotiations as such.

This question had, indeed, already been raised by the Planning Group. In its report (section 125), it emphasizes that ‚The Group would not support the incorporation into the Treaty of a list of the powers of the Union and would prefer to retain the present system, which specifies the legal basis of the Union's actions and policies in each case. Consequently, the Group declares itself in favour of retaining Article 235 as an instrument capable of adapting to the changing character inherent in the interpretation of the Union's objectives. Some members have proposed the introduction into Article 235 of a provision requiring the assent of the European Parliament.’

Furthermore, still in connection with Article 235, the interim report on the progress of the proceedings of the Intergovernmental Conference – presented by the Italian presidency to the European Council – indicates that ‚there seems to be a consensus in favour of retaining this article in the Treaty. It has, moreover, been generally accepted that the procedure currently laid down (unanimity and consultation with the European Parliament) should be retained’.

These two texts, of course, do not prejudge the final result of the Conference but do show that this question is under discussion there.

Be that as it may, if I may express a view on the subject of the opinion by the Court of Justice to which the honourable Member refers, I must emphasize that it does not relate to the enacting terms of Article 235 as a whole but is confined to the specific case of the Community's membership of the Human Rights Convention.

Question No 21 by Brian Crowley (H−0688/96)

Subject: Post offices

Has the Council undertaken an assessment of the potential impact of post office deregulation on the network of post offices in suburban and rural areas, villages and towns which provide a wide range of public services to local communities in many of the Member States?

Answer

As it has stated previously, in particular in its resolution of 7 February 1994 on the development of the Community postal services, the Council attaches great importance to ensuring the long‐term provision of a universal service in the postal sector. In that same text, the Council also emphasized that, in the course of laying down the regulations governing the sector, it would be necessary to take due account of the needs of users, including consumers, the interests of the workers in the postal sector and the contribution made by that sector to the economic, cultural and social development of the Community.

As the honourable Member knows, the Council is at present actively considering the proposal for a directive on this subject presented by the Commission, in order to reach a decision on a common position as soon as possible – and, if possible, in time for the ‚Telecommunications’ Council of 27 September 1996. As the Council has already reached a broad degree of consensus on the scope of the universal service, the central question now under discussion is the identification of those services suitable for reservation, including the timetable for the liberalization of some services.

In dealing with this question, which – as I have just said – is central to the Council's concerns, the latter, before deciding upon its common position, will certainly undertake a careful evaluation of the facts at its disposal regarding the economic, financial and social impact of the proposed measures.

Question No 22 by James Fitzsimons (H−0690/96)

Subject: Threats of increased contamination from THORP

The Radiological Protection Institute of Ireland has described as ‚disturbing, if true’, a report which appeared in the Guardian of 24 August 1996 which stated that British Nuclear Fuels might increase radioactive contamination from the THORP reprocessing plant in Cumbria as a result of plans to boost its flagging output.

Will the Council, under the Irish presidency, state what action it is pursuing to protest vigorously against any possible increase in radioactive emissions from THORP and will it outline what initiative it intends to take during its presidency in opposing the Sellafield/THORP operations?

Answer

As regards the operation of the Sellafield/THORP reprocessing plant in Cumbria to which the honourable Member refers, it is recalled that there are stringent safety standards in place in both the Community and national legislation to ensure protection of the population and the environment against dangers from ionizing radiation. In particular, attention is drawn to the fact that every Member State is subject to the provisions of Chapter III of the EURATOM Treaty on Health and Safety and the consequent Community legislation. These provisions require each Member State to take the necessary legislative, regulatory or administrative action to ensure compliance with the basic standards.

In this context, the recently adopted Council Directive 96/29/Euratom of 13 May 1996 laying down revised basic safety standards for the protection of the health of workers and the general public against the dangers arising from ionizing radiation has greatly reinforced the level of protection.

The Council can assure the honourable Member that it attaches the greatest importance to the protection of the health and safety of the European citizens.

The Belgian delegation has presented three joint actions aiming at countering these abominable crimes. Question No 24 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−0699/96)

Subject: OECD agreement on shipbuilding

A secret Council declaration was adopted on 19 December 1995 in connection with Council Regulation (EC) 3094/95(1) implementing the OECD agreement on aid to shipbuilding. This declaration seems to be in clear breach of the OECD agreement's ban on aid to the shipbuilding industry and of the Union's policy in the shipbuilding sector, cf. the ‚seventh shipyard directive’.

Will the Council provide an interpretation of the secret Council declaration and confirm that, once the OECD agreement is ratified, it will no longer be possible to grant state aid to the shipbuilding industry in the Union?

Answer

The honourable Member should note that, when Regulation (EC) No 3094/95 of 22 December 1995 on aid to shipbuilding was adopted, the Council did not approve any secret declaration on implementation of the OECD Agreement.

The Council would confirm that, following ratification of the Agreement concluded under the aegis of the OECD by all parties, direct aid to shipbuilding will be abolished with the exception of social assistance and research and development aid, which will be authorized up to certain specified ceilings.

However, indirect aid measures for shipbuilding in the form of credit facilities or loan guarantees for shipbuilders are authorized by the Agreement, provided that they comply with the OECD Understanding on Export Credits for Ships.

Question No 25 by Richard Howitt (H−0703/96)

Subject: Return of asylum‐seekers from the UK to other EU countries

What discussions have taken place and what is the view of the Council concerning new provisions under the UK's Immigration and Asylum Act for the return of asylum‐seekers from the UK to other EU countries in which they have first arrived from third countries? How far do reciprocal arrangements exist in other EU countries? Will the Council act to assist the situation of people like Ibush and Burim Gashi in my constituency, Bosnian refugees, who are denied reconciliation with other family members who have been granted asylum in other EU countries, and who are denied visas even to visit?

Answer

The matter raised in the first and second questions concerning new provisions under the UK's Immigration and Asylum Act for the return of asylum seekers from the UK to other EU countries, where they have first arrived, is dealt with in the Dublin Convention determining the State responsible for examining applications for asylum lodged in one of the Member States of the European Communities which will be ratified by all Member States in due course. There is no doubt that the UK legislation – as well as the legislation of other Member States – will be in line with the provisions of the Dublin Convention.

As to the third question, the Council is considering on the basis of relevant Commission proposals (i.e. the Monti Directives) the introduction of rules and facilities governing the free movement of third country nationals legally residing in a Member State of the European Union. However, no decision has been taken yet.

As to the specific aspect of family reunification, a resolution was adopted in 1993 with a view to harmonizing politics concerning family reunification. This resolution, however, does not deal with refugees recognized under the Geneva Convention. Preliminary work is at present carried out within the Third Pillar instances to see whether action on the EU‐level is needed. This preliminary work has shown that, in general, Member States foresee family reunification in their national legislation.

Finally, it may be recalled that it is not up to the Council to consider individual cases.

Question No 26 by Gerard Collins (H−0707/96)

Subject: Unused Structural Fund aid

In view of the reports that approximately 20 billion ECUS of Structural Fund aid allocated by the Commission over the last ten years has still not been utilized by the proposers of approved projects, does the Council have any plans to direct unused resources to projects which are ready to proceed, even if they are located in other Member States?

Answer

When the structural funds were reformed in 1993, the Council entrusted their management to the Commission. Specifically, the Commission decides how the resources available should be allocated among Member States on the basis of the criteria laid down in the basic legislation. The Commission also decides on the eligibility of the measures to be carried out in each Member State and on their funding.

It follows that the Council is not entitled to interfere in fund management.

Question No 27 by Pat Gallagher (H−0709/96)

Subject: EU aid to Siret orphanage in Romania

Is the Council in a position to provide an indication of the level of EU aid made available to date to help improve the situation of and conditions for children in orphanages in Romania and, in particular, the SIRET orphanage and does it agree that more aid needs to be provided?

Answer

The Council, like all the other institutions of the Union, has always been very aware of the problem represented by the situation of the orphanages in Romania. Community intervention in this respect began, incidentally, shortly after the fall of the Ceausescu regime, as soon as the Community became aware of the often tragic situation of the 650 institutions of this type existing in Romania.

I should like to make it clear at the outset that the Community activities in this field come within the ambit of the PHARE programme and are thus entirely administered by the Commission, to which the honourable Member should therefore apply if he requires detailed information. I shall confine myself to a broad outline of the present situation.

Following an initial stage in which it was necessary to supply genuine emergency aid designed to meet such fundamental needs as food, energy and heating in the orphanages, the Romanian government and the Commission felt that it was appropriate to devote their efforts to implementing a longer‐term and more comprehensive policy for the protection of children. This was the objective behind the setting‐up of the present programme, which covers the period 1994‐1996 and has a budget appropriation of ECU 12 million. This programme is geared to objectives such as:

‐ the implementation of training policies for personnel dealing with children;

‐ substitution schemes designed to limit the placement of children in these institutions;

‐ a new approach to the management of these institutions.

Some of the funds are still earmarked for the renovation of some institutions and for the supply of fuel oil, electricity and milk powder.

The Council has no information relating specifically to the SIRET orphanage and is not in a position to say whether the total amount of aid is or is not adequate. I will however remind the honourable Member that the priorities of the PHARE programme are determined in accordance with the needs and wishes of the beneficiary country, whose authorities cooperate closely with those responsible within the Commission.

Question No 28 by Alan Gillis (H−0711/96)

Subject: Human rights and democracy in Burma

Following the Council's condemnation of Burma on 15 July 1996 and specifically after the death in custody of Mr Leander Nichol, Honorary Consul, will the Council now consider the possibility of further concrete measures in view of the continuing oppression in that country?

Answer

Since the publication of an EU declaration on Burma on 5 July 1996 and the ensuing discussion at the GAC on 15/16 July, the EU has met with the Burmese Minister of Foreign Affairs, Ohn Gyaw, in the margin of the ASEAN Post‐Ministerial Conference in Jakarta (22 July 1996). The presidency reiterated verbally all the elements of the previous EU declaration, exhorting the Burmese authorities to provide a complete and satisfying follow‐up to all the points which were mentioned therein.

Minister Ohn Gyaw agreed to have further contacts through the missions of Myanmar in the EU and to a meeting with the EU Ministerial Troika in New York in the margin of the 51st UNGA.

The EU presidency made it clear that complete answers to the questions raised by the EU were expected by that date and that in the meantime the EU would go on monitoring closely the situation in Burma and discussing appropriate measures.

Question No 29 by Anne McIntosh (H−0480/96)

Subject: Extradition arrangements within the European Union

What steps is the Council taking to ensure a fair process of extradition between Member States when no extradition treaty exists between the Member States concerned?

Does the Council not agree that this is an area of great importance to the European citizen and one that should be tackled at European level?

Answer

The Council attaches particular importance to improving extradition between the Member States as this is an essential form of judicial cooperation between them both in conducting criminal proceedings and in carrying out sentences.

The Council would remind the honourable Member that nearly all Member States have ratified the European Convention on Extradition concluded under the aegis of the Council of Europe in 1957. That Convention already constitutes an important framework for facilitating extradition inter alia between the Member States.

Moreover, some Member States have concluded bilateral or multilateral agreements or arrangements between themselves which supplement the provisions of the 1957 Convention or facilitate application of the principles contained therein.

Following the entry into force of the Treaty on European Union, questions relating to extradition have been widely examined by Council bodies. In March 1995, the Council drew up a first Convention on simplified extradition procedure between the Member States of the European Union. That Convention simplifies the extradition procedure where the person who is the subject of the request does not oppose it.

These rules apply insofar as they are compatible with the fundamental principles of national law, including the European Convention for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms. The Convention on unified extradition was signed by all the Member States of the European Union in March 1995 and is currently being ratified at national level.

Member States have now completed negotiations on a second Convention relating to extradition. It also concerns the basic conditions for extradition, like the one drawn up in 1995, this Convention supplements the 1957 European Convention on Extradition. Its entry into force will make it possible to increase the number of cases which can result in extradition. Examples of this are the possibility of extradition in the case of an offence committed as part of a conspiracy or an association to commit offences, offences normally regarded as political offences and the possibility of extradition even where the person who is the subject of the request is a national of the requested Member State.

This Convention, which is an important stage in the strengthening of judicial cooperation between the Member States of the European Union, will probably be drawn up by the Council and signed by the Member States in September 1996.

***

II. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 44 by Ulf Holm (H−0701/96)

Subject: Future price stability objectives

If an economic and monetary union is set up within the EU, one of its principal tasks under the Treaty will be to achieve price stability. There is at present much debate in Sweden on EMU and its implications for our country, but opinions are divided on what the price stability objective actually involves. As this is one of the cornerstones of the EMU and the Treaty, I would ask the Commission:

Is there any more detailed level for price stability?

If so, who decide what that level is to be?

Will the price stability level then be fixed for evermore, or can it be adjusted in the light of the economic situation?

Answer

1. Stability of prices is a necessary condition for sustainable growth. Following the oil crisis of 1973, inflation rates rose swiftly in Europe, seriously undermining the resilience of the European economies and creating unemployment.

Although the Member States are no longer experiencing rates of inflation in double figures, it is important not to forget that the inflationary spiral, once begun, is extremely difficult to bring under control.

Inflation is a constant danger, not only to the competitiveness of business but also to the purchasing power of the less well‐off sections of the population.

2. Inflation for the Union as a whole has been successfully reduced from 5.6 % in the early 1990s to 2.6 % today. This reduction in inflation is laying the foundations of a sound, healthy economy which will allow determined and coordinated action to be taken against unemployment.

3. It is therefore reasonable for the Treaty to make price stability one of the fundamental economic principles of the Union, assigning this objective to the future ECB. Article 105 lays down that: ’The primary objective of the ESCB shall be to maintain price stability.’ That objective was deliberately not defined in arithmetical terms, in order to allow the monetary authorities a certain degree of discretion.

4. However, price stability is not the sole objective of economic policy. Without prejudice to that objective, the Treaty also specifies that the ESCB shall support the general economic policies in the Community with a view to helping the Community to carry out its objectives, as laid down in Article 2 of the Treaty.

5. These objectives include, in particular, as well as sustainable and non‐inflationary growth, a high degree of convergence of economic performance, and a high level of employment and of social protection.

In order to further the achievement of these objectives, the Treaty emphasizes that the economic policies of the Member States are a matter of common concern and shall be coordinated.

Responsibility for economic policy within the Union is thus shared between the ECB/ESCB, the national governments and also the social partners.

The latter, moreover, have acknowledged the validity of price stability by accepting the recommendations of the broad economic policy guidelines, which propose that wages should increase in a manner compatible with the objective of price stability so as to stimulate the creation of jobs.

***

Question No 45 by Richard Howitt (H−0704/96)

Subject: EMU and industry

What effect would delayed entry into the European Monetary Union have on British industry, particularly on trade within Europe, and how will the Commissioner ensure that countries who delay entry will not be penalized? Fifteen British industries have publicly voiced grave concerns over delayed entry; can you reassure them that such action by the British Government will not be detrimental?

Answer

1. The Commission has noted with interest the concerns recently expressed by fifteen large British undertakings. It understands their possible fears that they may not benefit from the advantages of the Euro as quickly as other undertakings.

2. Participation in economic and monetary union will represent a substantial economic gain for the participating States:

‐ Economic and monetary union will encourage stronger and more sustained growth.

‐ It involves a combination of economic conditions which are more favourable to job creation: a low rate of inflation enables purchasing power to be sustained; the reduction in public deficits will release savings to favour productive investment and will allow low interest rates.

‐ Economic and monetary union will eliminate the exchange rate fluctuations whose disastrous effects we have seen: the fluctuations of 1995 will have caused a loss of about 2 % of GDP growth and 1.5 million jobs for European economy.

‐ It will stimulate the proper functioning of the single market.

The Euro will also bring specific and immediate benefits to the undertakings themselves:

‐ The Euro will offer specific economies, especially in terms of transaction costs: undertakings and consumers will benefit to the extent of ECU 25, 000 to 30, 000 million per year.

‐ The Euro will also help to lighten the burden of operating charges on Europe's undertakings and will make it easier for them to compete.

‐ The Euro will place Europe's undertakings on the same footing as American ones by enabling them to sell and invest within a very large single market.

3. The Commission has, of course, no intention of discriminating against British business in the event that the United Kingdom were not to join the economic and monetary union immediately. The question is not so much that as whether British business will also be able to benefit, from the start, from the advantages of the Euro zone. The answer to that question is a matter for the British government.

***

Question No 46 by Jörn Svensson (H−0705/96)

Subject: EMU derogations

There seem at present to be three ways for an EU Member State not to take part in the EMU: Treaty opt‐outs (UK and Denmark), failing to meet the convergence criteria, and indefinitely delaying its decision, as the Swedish Minister of Finance indicated in an article in the newspaper Dagens Nyheter, which has attracted a considerable degree of attention.

The Swedish model of wait and see how the EMU turns out is based on the pronounced scepticism towards EMU among the Swedish public.

Does the Commission consider that the Swedish model is acceptable in Member States other than Sweden? Is there any ceiling to the number of countries which could follow the Swedish example without the whole EMU project being likely to collapse?

Answer

1. According to the Treaty on European Union, only two Member States benefit from an opting‐out clause: the United Kingdom and Denmark. As far as the others are concerned, the decision will be taken as early as possible in 1998 by the European Council, on the basis of the 1997 data.

The Commission does not share the honourable Member's doubts regarding the success of the project. The decision to move forward to the third phase of economic and monetary union on 1 January 1999 is irrevocable. The European Council meeting in Madrid confirmed that in December 1995.

2. The Commission is confident about the status of the process, as regards both convergence and the preparatory work. It has noted the progress made over the last 18 months:

‐ not only as regards the restructuring of the European economies: inflation was reduced from 3 % in 1995 to 2.6 % in 1996, the public deficit was reduced from 5.1 % to 4.4 % in 1996, and long‐term interest rates have fallen by more than 100 base points compared with the start of 1995;

‐ but also as regards the progress of the work necessary for the introduction of the Euro: the three files on the legal status of the Euro, the stability pact and the relation between the ‚ins’ and the ‚pre‐ins’ are likely to be the subject of a political agreement at the informal Council meeting in Dublin.

3. This leaves the problem of public opinion to which the honourable Member referred. In this respect, the Commission is glad to have the support of the European Parliament for the introduction and development of a priority information campaign which is to accompany the whole scenario regarding the introduction of the Euro. This campaign covers all the Member States, and will explain the arrangements for and advantages of the introduction of a single currency in Europe. The public in the Member States should rapidly be persuaded of the benefits of the Euro and of the need for their countries to join the economic and monetary union as soon as possible.

***

Question No 47 by Erich Schreiner (H−0713/96)

Subject: Monetary Union – convergence criteria

Which countries are unlikely to fulfil the convergence criteria for participation in Monetary Union?

What (extra) steps will the Commission take to ensure that they can nevertheless participate in Monetary Union?

What steps will the Commission take to help these countries to fulfil the convergence criteria?

Answer

1. The Commission does not wish to anticipate the stock‐taking operation which will be required for entry into the third phase of economic and monetary union.

In accordance with the decision reached by the Heads of State and Government in Madrid in December 1995, the European Council will take its decision as early as possible in 1998, on the basis of the reports by the Commission and the European Monetary Institute, and after consultation with the European Parliament.

The interim examination will be based only on the results for the year 1997.

As far as the Commission is concerned, therefore, it would be inappropriate to speculate on the likely dates, the more so since any such debate might prove counter‐productive by deflecting the Member States from their current restructuring efforts.

2. However, as it is improbable that all the Member States will be ready for 1 January 1999, the Commission is actively working to define a new monetary system which will be both a guarantee of monetary stability for the Union and an access door to the Euro for the ‚pre‐in’ countries.

Anchored centrally by the Euro, this new mechanism will have to be sufficiently flexible (with sufficiently wide fluctuation margins) and involve obligatory intervention at the margins and credit facilities.

Furthermore, in order to assist the ‚pre‐in’ States, as the honourable Member suggests, to join the Euro zone at the earliest possible moment, the Commission will also be taking steps to increase the efficiency of the multilateral surveillance procedures by instituting genuine ‚convergence contracts’ with those countries, which will be politically validated by the Ecofin Council thus assisting their efforts at restructuring.

In other words, the conditions of entry into economic and monetary union will be the same for all Member States of the Union, whether they be ‚ins’ or ‚pre‐ins’.

Question No 55 by Shaun Spiers (H−0671/96)

Subject: Environmental initiatives and the new millennium

Bearing in mind the dawning of the new millennium and the fact that environmental organizations across Europe are preparing campaigns to mark this significant date by emphasizing that we hold the planet in our trust for future generations, what plans does the Commission have to support environmental initiatives which look towards the third millennium, such as the Millennium Tree Line?

Answer

Until now the Commission has not yet been able to define its proposals for a Community action on the occasion of the turn of the Millennium. During the coming months, the Commission will charge a special Task Force of the preparation of an Action Programme ‚3rd Millennium’ which has to take into account the initiatives planned by the Member States on this occasion. In early 1997 the Commission plans to submit a Communication to Council and Parliament on this matter which will include a provision for expenditure. During the second semester of 1997 the Commission will be able, on this basis, to prepare the Commission's own initiatives and to make a selection of the initiatives it can associate itself with.

The Commission can assure that, in this framework, the Community care for the environment will obviously be highlighted and that the Commission will give thorough consideration to initiatives like the Millennium Tree Line.

Question No 56 by Marie‐Paule Kestelijn‐Sierens (H−0675/96)

Subject: Conclusions of the working party on health‐care waste under the Priority Waste Stream Project

Has the Commission taken note of the conclusions of the working party on health‐ care waste set up under the Priority Waste Stream Project? Will the Commission act on the recommendations that the working part sets out in its conclusions? Does the Commission not consider it necessary – having regard to the flow of traffic that results from the wide discrepancies between Member States in the cost of processing health‐care waste and the attendant pressures on the environment – to propose a harmonized policy for this category of waste as soon as possible? If so, will the Commission accept the working party's recommendations on including a definition of dangerous health‐care waste in the European dangerous wastes list? If not, what other action will it take in response to this study?

Answer

1. The Commission has taken due note of the conclusion of the Working Group on Health‐Care Wastes which was set up as part of the project for controlling the flows of priority wastes.

2. The Commission will be following the recommendations made by the Working Group in its conclusions. It is currently developing and evaluating the various alternative schemes which may be considered.

3. Having noted, from the conclusions of the project group, that there are differences in the definitions and costs of processing of health‐care wastes in the various Member States, the Commission is examining the degree of harmonization which it is reasonable to propose in this matter.

4. The Commission has taken no decision as yet regarding the course of action it will be proposing in this matter.

Question No 57 by Eryl McNally (H−0683/96)

Subject: Keeping and use of primates

Would the Commission agree that a working party, including animal welfare representatives and experts from industry and research, should be set up to advise the Commission on a policy for the keeping and use of primates, both those raised in captivity and those caught in the wild?

Answer

The Commission considers that at this stage it is not possible to decide on the creation of a working group to advise on the keeping and use of primates, both captive bred and wild caught.

The European Conference on ‚50 % Reduction in the Use of Laboratory Animals by the year 2000’, to be held in Brussels in 1997 will raise the question, amongst others, of the use of primates. The Commission will review the results of this conference, and will examine the possibility of a suitable advisory or working group to examine the subject in more detail.

Nonetheless, despite the inability to give an immediate response to this subject, the Commission is conscious of the importance of this issue, and it is a subject that is regularly discussed in its meetings with the National Competent Authorities for the application of Directive 86/609/EEC concerning laboratory animals protection.

***

Question No 58 by Anita Pollack (H−0684/96)

Subject: Use of primates for research purposes

Would the Commission explain why so little progress has been made towards developing a coherent European Union policy for the use of primates caught in the wild in research and for the housing conditions of primates in captive breeding centres and research establishments?

Answer

According to Article 7, paragraphs 2 and 3, Article 19, paragraph 4 and Article 21 of Directive 86/609/EEC, it is required in the Community to use purpose‐bred rather than wild‐caught animals in experimentation. This applies in particular to the use of primates. Nevertheless, in certain cases a derogation may be granted, for example due to the suitability of the animal or lack in availability of purpose‐bred animals. In these cases authorization for the use of wild caught animals lies in the hands of the national authorities.

The European Congress on the Ethics of Animal Experimentation sponsored by the Commission will take place in Brussels on 17/18 December 1996. Amongst the subjects that the Congress will deal with are Animal Research and Animal Protection and the use of primates in experiments.

The European Conference on ‚50 % Reduction in the Use of Laboratory Animals by the year 2000’, to be held in Brussels in 1997, will also raise the question of the use of primates. On this occasion, the Commission will consider the steps to be taken regarding their conditions of housing and care.

***

Question No 59 by James Fitzsimons (H−0691/96)

Subject: Threats of increased contamination from THORP

The Radiological Protection Institute of Ireland has described as ‚disturbing, if true’, a report which appeared in the Guardian of 24 August 1996 which stated that British Nuclear Fuels might increase radioactive contamination from the THORP reprocessing plant in Cumbria as a result of plans to boost its flagging output.

Has the Commission investigated this claim, and what action does it intend to take to protect Irish citizens against the threat of increased radioactive emissions from Sellafield?

Answer

The United Kingdom government is responsible for ensuring the application of the Community basic safety standards for the health protection of the general public and workers against the dangers of ionizing radiation(2) and hence for issuing radioactive discharge authorizations. In so doing it has to ensure that the Community limits for exposure of members of the public are respected.

The present Sellafield authorizations were issued subsequent to the Commission's opinion under the terms of Article 37 of the Euratom Treaty relating to the plan to bring THORP into operation(3) . According to the information available to the Commission no request for amendments to these authorizations has yet been submitted to the relevant United Kingdom authorities.

Question No 60 by Irene Crepaz (H−0696/96)

Subject: Natural habitats

In the Commission's opinion, do ‚natural habitat types of Community interest’ (within the meaning of Council Directive 92/43/EEC)(4) enjoy protection even where they have not been notified?

Does the construction of a power station in such a ‚natural habitat’ contravene existing Community law in principle?

Environmental organizations complain that some areas in Austria deserving of protection were not notified to the Commission. What action does the Commission plan to take in this regard?

Answer

1. The Commission holds the view that there are at least two cases in which natural habitats of Community interest benefit from protection under Article 6 of Directive 92/43/EEC without having been included in a ‚national list’ pursuant to Article 4(1) of this Directive.

‐ Sites which are subject to the consultation procedure under Article 5 of Directive 92/43/EEC are protected under Article 6(2) of this Directive during the consultation period and pending a Council decision.

‐ Sites which are identical with areas that should be classified as special protection areas pursuant to Directive 79/409/EEC benefit from protection according to the principles established by the European Court of Justice in its judgement of 2 August 1993 (Case C−355/90, Santona). The Commission holds the view that after the transposition deadline for Directive 92/43/EEC, if an important bird site benefits from the Santona principle, it benefits from it in the sense of Article 6(2), (3) and (4) of that Directive.

2. The question whether the construction of a power plant in a natural habitat of Community interest is in conflict with the Community legislation in force, in particular Directive 92/43/EEC, cannot be answered in the abstract. However, it should be noted that Article 6(2) and (3) of this Directive provide, in respect of special areas of conservation hosting priority natural habitat types, for a procedure in which a weighing of interests and a consultation of the Commission has to take place for projects having negative effects on that site. The realization of any such project without first consulting the Commission would therefore seem to be problematic under the above‐mentioned provisions.

3. The Commission has decided to open infringement procedures under Article 169 of the EC Treaty against all Member States, including Austria, which to date have not submitted complete national lists pursuant to Article 4(2) of Directive 92/43/EEC.

Question No 61 by MaLou Lindholm (H−0702/96)

Subject: Environmental impact of power cables across the Baltic

Vattenfall, the Swedish power undertaking, is planning together with other interested parties to lay a high‐voltage DC power line from Karlshamn to Dunowo in Poland. This will create powerful magnetic fields and produce chlorine (5‐20 tonnes a year) in particular around the negative pole of the installation which will be located at a very sensitive spot in the archipelago, inter alia one of the best breeding grounds in the Baltic for the greatly threatened wild Baltic salmon. As the Visby Summit this summer adopted a document entitled ‚To promote environmentally sound energy production and distribution...’ and as the Commission, as the chairman of HELCOM, has spoken of a vigorous campaign against pollution in the Baltic, how far does the Commission consider that the project complies with the ESBO Convention, and has an environment impact assessment been completed under EU rules? How does the project relate to the relevant EU directives, in particular the habitat and wild birds directives? What action does the Commission intend to take to ensure that the project complies with these international conventions?

Answer

The project as described by the honourable Member refers to transport of energy via a submarine cable from Sweden to Poland. As far as Environmental Impact Assessment is concerned, this type of project is covered neither by the ESBO Convention nor by the Directive 85/337/EEC on the assessment of the effects of certain public and private projects on the environment.

As far as nature conservation is concerned, on the basis of available information, the Community nature directives cannot be applied either, because there is no designated area on the Community territory and because only the fresh water habitat of the salmon is protected according to the Habitats Directive 92/43/EEC.

Question No 62 by Marianne Eriksson (H−0706/96)

Subject: Radioactive discharges at Dounreay

It is clear from Commissioner Bjerregaard's answer to my written question (E−1583/96) that the Commission has no intention of taking action to seek to stop the planned increase of radioactive discharges at the Dounreay plant in Scotland. The Commissioner states that the doses received by the most exposed members of the local population must remain within the limits set in Community legislation and that the radioactive discharges will have negligible impact on the west coast of Sweden.

What are the limits for the radioactive exposure of the local population under Community legislation?

What is the factual basis for the Commissioner's claim that the discharges will have no impact on the west coast of Sweden?

Answer

The current annual limits of exposure for members of the public are laid down in Council Directive 80/836/EURATOM(5) as modified by Directive 84/467/EURATOM(6) , the principal limit being five millisievert per year for whole body exposure. These directives will be superseded by Directive 96/29/EURATOM(7) which will come into force in the year 2000. The new Directive will, inter alia, reduce that limit to one millisievert per year.

Member States governments are of course responsible in the first instance for ensuring compliance with Council directives. In the present case regular United Kingdom government reports have demonstrated compliance in the Dounreay region with both the current and the future annual limit for exposure of members of the public. Research work has demonstrated that pollution from that region will undergo major dilution during its dispersion across the North Sea, resulting in a correspondingly lower exposure of members of the public in Scandinavia relative to that in the Dounreay region. On this basis it can be stated that exposure of members of the public in Scandinavia is of no significance.

Question No 63 by Per Stenmarck (H−0618/96)

Subject: Further discharges of oil into the Baltic

According to the answer given by Commissioner Kinnock on 16 June 1995 to Written Question No E−1170/95(8) , the Commission was carrying out a study during 1995 on the enforcement in Community waters of safety and environmental rules.

Has that study resulted in any practical proposals as to how to deal with the discharges of oil that are still occurring in the Baltic?

Answer

The study on ‚The enforcement in EC waters of safety and environmental rules’ includes a number of recommendations which are now under consideration within the Commission. Suggestions relating to waste management on board ship and in ports will also be discussed with the Member States and the industry.

Moreover, specific measures with regard to the development of EC legislation for the provision and use of shore reception facilities are already envisaged in the Commission's Common Transport Policy Action Programme 19952000.

So far as the Baltic Sea is concerned, the Commission is actively participating in developments under the Helsinki Convention aimed at improving protection of the marine environment in the Baltic Sea.

Finally, the honourable Member will be aware that the Directive on Port State Control entered into force on 1.7.96. Its objective is to establish common criteria for control of ships by the port State and the harmonization of procedures on inspection and detention. The implementation of this Directive should result in an increase in compliance with international legislation such as SOLAS and MARPOL, ensuring a better protection of the marine environment in European waters including the Baltic.

Question No 64 by Jens‐Peter Bonde (H−0621/96)

Subject: Access by the Court of Auditors and Parliament's Budgetary Control Committee to vouchers

Will the Commission give the Court of Auditors and Parliament's Budgetary Control Committee access to vouchers for all payments from the EAGGF?

Answer

The Commission does not have the information on EAGGF payment referred to by the honourable Member. All documentation regarding each EAGGF payment is kept by the Member States. The role of the Commission as far as EAGGF payments are concerned is to pay the advances to the competent national administrations, which are responsible for the management of payments to the final beneficiaries on the basis of the payment requests they receive. However, it may be emphasized that it is already the Member States' duty to give the auditing bodies of the Community access to all relevant documents related to EAGGF payments.

Question No 65 by Sören Wibe (H−0622/96)

Subject: Organization and administration of the Commission

In Dagens Politik of 5 July 1996, a Swedish Deputy Director‐General of DG III commented on the work of the Commission and was highly critical of the way in which the Commission is organized and administered, especially of the fact that:

Many studies are commissioned which then find their way straight into someone's drawer; objectives are not set for work, which is also not followed up when completed;

Acceptance of responsibility and delegation are virtually unknown concepts: a mission has to go through perhaps ten intermediaries before being submitted to a Director‐General for approval; the same work on particular cases and studies may be carried out in various sections of the Commission, with the officials responsible jealously guarding their preserves, so that it is difficult to achieve a reasonable overall result.

The Deputy Director‐General did, however, also make a number of suggestions in the interview as to how the aforementioned problems could be tackled. Is the Commission working on ways to tackle such problems, which are unfortunately all too common in bureaucratic environments.

Answer

As laid down in the EC Treaty, the Commission consists of twenty Members who act collectively, with decisions taken on the joint responsibility of all its Members. This organizational structure together with the differing fields of its competence and the multicultural environment (fifteen different nationalities, speaking eleven different languages) makes for a rather complex administration. The challenge is to administer this complexity in a nonbureaucratic and transparent way.

Several important initiatives have been launched recently to improve efficiency and performance. The programme to promote Sound and Efficient Management (SEM 2000) spearheaded by Commissioners Liikanen and Gradin, seeks to improve the quality of the Commission's administration over time. Improvements under way include:

‐ the establishment of annual work priorities for the Commission;

‐ the reinforcement of the financial management structure within each Directorate‐General;

‐ the provision of continuous evaluation of the effectiveness of current and future programmes of work;

‐ the establishment of the true cost of any action in both financial and human resource terms;

‐ the examination of horizontal procedures and methods with a view to their simplification.

It also encourages decentralization and delegation within the services and tries to maintain a balance between scope for individual action and the global responsibility to avoid duplication, waste or fraud through increased levels of transparency and coordination. The example of studies, quoted by the honourable Member, is a case in point. Here each Director‐General is responsible for their own studies budget while the inherent risk of duplication is countered by the circulation, coordinated by the Secretariat General, of all studies programmes planned by the different services.

Question No 66 by Rui Vieira (H−0627/96)

Subject: Community preference

The conclusion of cooperation agreements between the European Union and third countries often affects products or productivity in some Member States or in regions of Member States, with a resultant increase in unemployment, which is particularly acute at present. This is too great a burden for those countries and regions in the European Union which are still coping with a great many problems.

In certain areas (e.g. certain farm products and ‚television without frontiers’) market quotas have been laid down or attempts have been made to establish them, in order to protect Union producers. This principle of Community preference needs to be extended and reinforced.

Does the Commission not consider that studies, measures and action as regards Community preference need to be developed and taken further so as to protect the economy and employment in the Member States, especially the weakest and poorest countries?

Answer

The Commission entirely shares the view of the honourable Member as to the importance of respecting the principle of Community preference.

Community preference must be understood in its broadest sense – creating the best possible conditions to ensure that Community products find an outlet on the markets.

That is why, in the agricultural strategy document presented at the Madrid Council meeting in December 1995, the Commission emphasized the necessary competitiveness of Community agriculture in a context of increasing competition, both on the export markets and on the Community market itself.

Question No 67 by Mair Morgan (H−0630/96)

Subject: Odorization of gases – protection of the consumer

Are there any current EU laws covering Community‐wide practice of adding odours to gases to make the identification of leaking gas easier?

In particular I am concerned about the odorization of propane and butane gases which are used domestically. What regulations exist within the Community concerning the odorization of these gases?

Answer

The odorization of gases – in particular of propane and butane – to facilitate the detection of possible leaks has not, as such, been the subject of any Community legislation.

In the various Member States, odorization involves compliance with a law, a standard or a practice.

In all cases, butane and propane gases for domestic use, as marketed in the various Member States, have, according to information received, a characteristic odour which allows possible leaks to be detected. This odour may be natural or may result from the presence of additives, depending on the production method.

Question No 68 by Wolfgang Nußbaumer (H−0631/96)

Subject: Cohesion Fund – combating fraud

In 1996 ECU 2 443 700 000 has been allocated to the Cohesion Fund for redistribution to the Member States, and in 1997 the sum will increase to ECU 2 748 700 000. In both years ECU 300 000 has been earmarked for the budget item dealing with measures to combat fraud.

What estimate can be given of the annual losses suffered by the Cohesion Fund as a result of fraud?

How high is the success rate in detecting instances of attempted fraud and how much money is thus saved from misappropriation?

Answer

The Cohesion Fund only makes available financial resources which are linked to projects in the fields of transport and protection of the environment. The linking of contributions to specific projects and the precautions in terms of monitoring and control should make the Cohesion Fund little susceptible to fraud in the narrow sense.

In any event, it is up to the four Member States concerned in the first instance to monitor the proper use of appropriations from the Cohesion Fund and to notify the Commission, in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 1831/94, of cases of fraud or irregularities. The Member States have not yet notified any such cases. For its part, the Commission will remain vigilant, so that no opportunity for fraud arises in the future.

Question No 69 by John McCartin (H−0632/96)

Subject: Satellite surveillance and fisheries protection

Can the Commission inform Parliament whether the proposal on satellite surveillance for fishery protection is making any progress and will the Commission indicate by how much the cost of fishery protection could be reduced by introducing the surveillance system?

Answer

The Commission has made a proposal for a Council Regulation on the establishment of a satellite‐based monitoring system for Community fishing vessels (COM(96) 232 final of 28.05.1996). Such a system would enable Member States to locate their fishing vessels at any time.

The Commission is proposing that for an initial period starting on 1 January 1997 the system would be applied to Community vessels operating in sensitive fisheries. Subsequently, from 1 January 1999, the system would be applied to all other Community vessels of more that 15 metres in length.

The proposal was presented by the Commission to the Council on 10 June 1996. Fisheries Ministers agreed to delay a decision until the end of the year. In the meantime, the proposal is being examined at working party level.

During 1994/1995, Member States carried out pilot projects for satellite monitoring, involving up to 350 vessels throughout the Community. The pilot projects were funded with ECU 10 million from the Community budget.

On the basis of experience gained with these projects, the Commission considers that satellite‐based monitoring of fishing vessels will considerably improve the effectiveness of fisheries control. The increase in the efficiency of the deployment of aircraft and patrol vessels alone would justify the cost of satellite monitoring.

Expenditure would normally be eligible under Council Decision 95/527/EC on a Community financial contribution towards certain expenditure incurred by the Member States implementing the monitoring and control systems applicable to the Common Fisheries Policy(9)

Question No 70 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0635/96)

Subject: ’Cultural’ or ‚conference’ centre or opera which is being built in Freedom Park

In its reply to my Question No H−0143/96(10) concerning the Athens Music Centre, the Commission stated that the conference centre infrastructure projects financed by the European Union included activities specifically mentioned in the Greek law on the establishment of the Athens Music Centre. The Commission will undoubtedly be aware that, for the infrastructure projects in question, the difference in appellation between the ‚conference centre’ referred to in CSF Programme 7.2 and the ‚cultural centre’ referred to in Presidential Decree (Government Bulletin 1061D/4.12.1995) is very significant, because in the first instance the approval of the Greek Tourist Board is required and other procedures must be fulfilled, but not in the second. It is also alleged in the press that instead of the so‐called ‚conference centre’, what is in fact being built is an opera; the evidence for this is the architectural plan approved by Authorization No 702/96.

Will the Commission say whether it intends to examine the reasons which led to the difference in appellation of the same project in the Greek Presidential Decree and in the CSF, so as to avoid a situation in which an unscheduled project which does not meet the legal requirements is funded, and whether it will examine allegations that the project under construction is neither the ‚cultural’ centre (the Greek authorities), nor the ‚conference’ centre (the Community authorities) but may in fact turn out to be an opera?

Answer

The creation of the Music Hall of Athens was decided in 1956. The plan included a large concert hall of 2, 0002, 500 places, a recital hall of 1, 0‐1, 200 places, a large music library, a foyer and offices and provision for open air concerts.

In 1981, by Law 1298/81, a contract was established between the Greek State and the cultural organizations involved in the management of the music hall for the creation of OMMA (Organization of the Music Hall of Athens). Article 2 of this law stipulated that ‚the rooms of the Music Hall will be used for musical and cultural events and for the organization of conferences’. On 4.12.1995 (Greek O.J. D1061), the extension of the music hall rooms for cultural events was agreed; it is clear that this extension can be used for all music hall purposes including conferences.

In the light of these considerations it can be concluded that the creation of a cultural and conference centre is legal. The agreement of other organizations is not necessary.

As mentioned in the answer to oral question H−0426/96, the Commission is cofinancing under the Structural Funds the infrastructure necessary for the organization of conferences and the treatment of the environment in the surrounding park.

Concerning the items in the Greek press concerning the creation of an opera house instead of a conference and cultural centre, construction permit no. 702/96 concerns the construction of the extension mentioned above and not of an opera house.

Question No 71 by Gary Titley (H−0636/96)

Subject: Article 6(1) of Directive 76/768/EEC – labelling of ingredients and function on cosmetics products

The Commission recently informed me that 11 Member States had not so far communicated their progress in implementing the article of the Directive referred to above(11) which is due to come into force on 1 January 1997. Consequently, does the Commission have any plans to ask the Member States to make faster progress in this area which is of great importance to many consumers?

Answer

Basic Directive 76/768//EEC on the harmonization of the legislations of the Member States on cosmetics products came into force on 30 January 1978.

This directive has been amended on six occasions, the most recent amendments dating back to Council Directive 93/35. That directive, in principle, came into force on 14 June 1995. However, pursuant to Article 2 of that directive, it is only from 1 January 1997 that the Member States will be required to take the necessary steps to ensure that manufacturers and importers established in the Community cease to market cosmetics products which fail to comply with the provisions of the directive.

The Commission is, as usual, monitoring the proper transposition of the Community texts and compliance with them, depending on the specific cases.

Otherwise, the honourable Member is asked to refer to the answer given to his written question 1538/96 on the same subject.

Question No 72 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0643/96)

Subject: Failure by certain Member States to meet their obligations to reduce their fleets (MGP III) and repercussions on MGP IV

With a view to the forthcoming proposal to reduce the Community fleet contained in MGP IV and in the light of the general repercussions of this issue,

Which are the ‚dilatory’ Member States that have not reduced their fleets in accordance with the requirements of MGP III (with details and extent of failure to comply)?

Will these reductions in fishing capacity which have not yet been implemented by certain Member States be added to MGP IV, since by not complying with MGP III these states have seriously damaged the Union's entire policy of conservation of fishery resources?

Answer

A number of Member States will probably not entirely fulfil their obligations to reduce excess fleet capacity as laid down in MAGP III. However, it is too early to be able to say this definitely, as MAGP III will not end until 31 December 1996. The results achieved by the Member States by 31 December 1995 were the subject of a report to the European Parliament and to the Council. That report(12) gives details of the extent to which each Member State has complied with its obligations.

The Commission shares the honourable Member's view that, within the framework of its draft decision on the new MAGP IV programme, due account must be taken of the efforts made in the past when defining the new objectives. Under these conditions, Member States which have failed to reduce their fleets sufficiently will first have to make good that shortfall, while Member States that have exceeded their objectives will benefit from an initial credit.

Question No 74 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0655/96)

Subject: Foot‐and‐mouth disease in Greece

Foot‐and‐mouth disease in Greece is usually found only in border regions. According to reliable records this is due to the low standard of the veterinary monitoring of animals in the neighbouring countries where foot‐and‐mouth disease is almost endemic. It is estimated that if the European Union made available funds for veterinary monitoring and prevention this would benefit not only neighbouring states, but also the economy of the European Union and its Member States. Is the EU in a position to use MEDA or PHARE funds for this purpose? Will it propose, for example, a vaccination programme for border regions to prevent outbreaks of food‐and‐mouth disease?

Answer

The economic consequences of outbreaks of foot and mouth disease (FMD) in the Community are severe and funds have been made available to prevent FMD entering the Community from neighbouring countries.

Within the Phare Programme the Commission has prepared a multi‐country veterinary diagnosis and control programme. The objective of this programme is to improve the control of epidemic diseases such as foot and mouth disease in Eastern European countries. A main component of the programme is support to diagnostic laboratories engaged in the monitoring of animal diseases.

During 1996 the Commission has adopted two decisions concerning the supply of vaccines to countries neighbouring Greece. A total of 600, 000 doses have been made available for Albania and 250, 000 for the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia.

In cooperation with the FAO, a special foot and mouth disease mission will visit Turkey in September and October this year. The mission will evaluate measures to reduce the risk of spreading foot and mouth disease from Turkey to the Community.
