Question No 75 by Yannos Kranidiotis (H−0661/96)

Subject: Statements by Tansu Çiller on new customs tariffs which Turkey intends to levy on imports

According to the Deputy Prime Minister of Turkey, Mrs Tansu Çiller, Turkey intends to impose new customs tariffs of 6 % on imports.

The Agreement on Customs Union signed by Turkey on 6 March 1995 provides, inter alia, that the contracting parties may not impose customs tariffs or taxes on bilateral trade and are obliged to implement new customs tariffs in their relations with third countries.

Turkey's decision to impose new tariffs is therefore contrary to the commitments undertaken by Turkey vis‐à‐vis the European Union on the implementation of the Agreement on Customs Union.

Will the Commission say what sanctions it intends to impose should the Turkish Government go ahead with its plans to increase tariffs?

Answer

As soon as the Commission was informed of the decision taken by the Central Bank of Turkey on 22 July 1996 to introduce a new tax, it asked for explanations from the Turkish authorities and undertook a review of the measure in question.

A meeting was organized by the Commission's representation in Ankara on 30 July 1996, to be attended by the Member States and the Turkish authorities so that the latter could explain the content of this new tax.

This 6 % tax is, in actual fact, a tax on credit in foreign currencies contracted in Turkey. Before this new tax was adopted, only a credit contracted in Turkey in Turkish pounds was the subject of a tax. The Turkish authorities wanted to eliminate this discrimination against the national currency by extending the tax to credit in foreign currencies.

This tax, then, is not reintroducing customs duties on Turkish imports and is not discriminatory in nature. The Commission is, however, still verifying that this measure conforms to the provisions on indirect taxation contained in the decision on customs union.

Question No 76 by Hilde Hawlicek (H−0663/96)

Subject: Role of public service broadcasting

In a resolution adopted in December 1994 at the Council of Europe ministerial conference on mass media policy, the Union Ministers with sole responsibility for public service broadcasting unanimously and emphatically underlined the importance of public service broadcasting for the political values and social and legal structures of our democratic social order. From this point of view they found that it was more necessary than ever to guarantee the existence and further development of public service broadcasting for our society. Your frequently repeated statements on public service broadcasting – most recently at the press conference to present the annual report of the Information Society Forum – are in stark contrast to the role of public service broadcasting as defined in all Member States of the Union: namely to inform, entertain and educate the whole population (irrespective of status or income) impartially at all times with a full programme.

How do you now justify your statements that public service broadcasting in its present form is over‐privileged, inefficient and even largely superfluous? What role do you see at all for public service broadcasting, equipped with rights and obligations, in the information society – for the definition of which you are responsible within the remit of the Commission?

Answer

The development of the information society is already well under way. It should be noted that in the information society traditional broadcasting is as important as the growing forms of interactive individual communication and mass communication.

Private and public service broadcasters both face great challenges in this emerging context. Whether the dual systems of private and public broadcasters chosen by most of the Member States can assert themselves in the multi‐media market will depend largely upon the broadcasters themselves. The Commission is supportive of all actors involved in the transition to the information society. At the same time, the Commission must ensure that public funding of public service broadcasters does not distort competition, and it must prevent the abuse or strengthening of a dominant position arising.

With the constant stream of new technological advances the mass media can be expected to become ever more differentiated. The media will offer ever‐greater possibilities and increasingly sophisticated services. Whilst it must prevent discrimination and distortion of competition, the Commission is supportive of all economic actors who sense the opportunities offered by the information society and are adapting themselves to benefit from it.

Question No 77 by Mark Watts (H−0665/96)

Subject: Flight crew and cabin crew work and rest time regulations, and common training and licensing standards for flight and cabin crews

Will the Commission now report back on:

The results and progress of the Forum on Flight and Duty Time, held on 11 June 1996, as the three months have passed since the resolution of March 1996(13) . The action it proposes to take in response to these results, and on the timetable in place to bring forth proposals on flight and duty time for aircrew as a matter of urgency. The current status and the text of JAR‐OPs1 subpart Q within the JAA, and on the progress it has made in bringing forward its proposal for common training and licensing standards for aircrew. Progress on the formation of a Community Civil Aviation Authority.

Answer

The Commission's follow‐up report to Parliament's resolution is currently being finalised and will be made available to Parliament in due course, in line with existing practice.

As a result of the Forum, a small Task Force composed of employer and employee representatives, Aviation Authorities and Scientific experts was set up to evaluate the current situation and assist the Commission in drawing up proposals on flight and duty time for aircrew. The Task Force held its first meeting on 1 August 1996 and further meetings are scheduled. The Commission also intends to undertake two studies to ascertain the economic and safety impact of the existing JAA proposals. Information and recommendations from these studies will be used by the Commission in its own proposals as soon as feasible.

The current JAR OPs Subpart Q proposals were withdrawn from the package of requirements adopted by the JAA in March 1995 and have not yet been adopted.

With regard to Commission proposals for common training and licensing standards for pilots, the Commission is examining the JAR‐FCL, which is expected to be formally adopted by JAA in October 1996. The Commission continues working on the harmonisation of flight crew training and licensing, and will follow the discussion on this subject very closely with a view to presenting its own proposals in the near future.

Proposals for common safety training standards for cabin crew are also scheduled to be adopted by the Commission in the near future.

With regard to the setting up of a European Civil Aviation Authority, the Commission continues to work on a draft recommendation for a Council decision authorizing it to start negotiations to conclude an international convention establishing a European organization responsible for civil aviation safety. A Commission recommendation to the Council is expected before the end of 1996.

‐ Mountain Risk Engineering (with ICIMOD)(16) 965 000

‐ Other activities are on‐going in the areas of Refugees (Bhutanese and Tibetan), Human Rights, NGOs, AIDS, Drugs, Hides and Skins.

2. The Joint Commission set up by the Agreement will meet in principle every other year, alternatively in Brussels and in Katmandu.

It shall be composed by senior officials responsible for the various areas covered by the Agreement.

This should permit that the functioning and implementation of the Agreement be properly monitored and ensured.

The Joint Commission will be at the hub of the Agreement implementation and it should be the forum for a dialogue on Community‐Nepal cooperation especially on major political issues.

Specialised subgroups set up by the Joint Commission will monitor the performance of the various cooperation activities foreseen in the Agreement and they will report to the Joint Commission.

The first Joint Commission is planned to meet in Katmandu on 18‐19 November 1996.

The Joint Commission is prepared in Brussels by the Commission in cooperation with the Member States represented in the specialised working groups of the Council.

Question No 79 by Gérard Caudron (H−0668/96)

Subject: Imports of textile products and economic development

The signing of the GATT agreements giving rise to the WTO (World Trade Organization) was intended to reduce customs barriers and promote international trade.

In the textile industry, as in others, it has exacerbated wage and social disparities between the developed and developing countries.

Relocations to developing countries because of their low wage and social costs have resulted in the closure of many textile businesses and the loss of millions of jobs in Europe, without at the same time helping the developing countries.

In the face of these relocations and competitive devaluations there is an urgent need to protect the European textile sector and encourage the development of the ACP countries by raising their standard of living.

Could the Commission not consider the application of an economic and social development levy on imports of textile products from developing countries or those with low labour costs, this being paid back to the exporting countries and reallocated partly for development and partly to the importers to finance their social security schemes to make up for the loss of wage contributions as a result of the imports?

Answer

The Commission is fully aware of the difficulties which are facing the European textiles and clothing industries. As highlighted by the recent Commission communication on ‚the impact of international developments on the textiles and clothing sector’ these difficulties are caused by many factors: declining consumption on the domestic Community market, high domestic labour costs, as well as increased competition from imports.

In response to these challenges the Commission has already taken a wider range of initiatives such as the RETEX programme aimed at assisting regions dependent on textiles production, as well as establishing programmes in the fields of research and development, technological innovation, industrial cooperation, management training and export promotion.

Both the Community and Member States provide considerable financial assistance to developing countries but certain of these countries have made clear that it is access to the Community market that they consider as the best method to help ensure their development. To this end the Community grants preferential access for developing countries' exports within the framework of its generalised tariff preferences scheme (GSP). However, due to the sensitivity of textile and clothing items, the preferential margin granted for these products is much lower than that for other sectors.

This sensitivity is the reason why textiles and clothing are the only industrial sectors (with the exception of certain non‐textile quotas maintained vis‐à‐vis China) where the EC maintains quantitative restrictions on imports. With WTO members textiles trade is governed by the provisions of GATT 1994 and more specifically the Agreement on Textiles and Clothing (ATC). With non‐WTO members and preferential countries trade is governed by bilateral agreements which have been negotiated by the Commission on behalf of the Community.

The Commission accepts that developing countries have legitimate comparative advantages in terms of an abundant workforce and lower wages. At the same time one of the main objectives of the Community's development policy is to help developing countries establish living and working conditions which are in conformity with internationally recognized human rights and working standards. This is primarily done through cooperation agreements but the Commission may also use trade policy to this end – in so far as this is compatible with the Community's international commitments.

Regarding the honourable Member's suggestion of a ‚social and development levy’ on imports of textiles and clothing from developing countries this would run counter to the Most Favoured Nation principle of the GATT and is therefore incompatible with the Community's international commitments.

***

Question No 80 by Robert Sturdy (H−0669/96)

Subject: Quality beef assurance schemes

As part of the programme of measures taken by the UK to eradicate BSE and to restore confidence to the beef market, the UK is currently drafting legislation to introduce a quality been assurance scheme.

Does the Commission agree that a quality assurance scheme introduced at the Community level would also have a positive effect in restoring confidence in the market for beef throughout the Union?

Answer

A beef assurance scheme is currently being established by the British Government as a way of identifying herds which have not had a case of BSE and in which meat and bonemeal has not been used in the feed. Animals over 30 months coming from these herds will be exempt from the normal rule that cattle over the age of 30 months in the UK may not be used for food or animal feed. The conclusions of the Florence Summit referred to such a programme as part of a package aimed at a step by step lifting of the ban on UK cattle and products.

It is considered that such a system will make a major contribution to the reestablishment of consumer confidence in beef in the UK which has a high incidence of BSE. However, the incidence of BSE in other affected Member States is extremely low and it is not considered necessary to set up a similar programme in these countries. There would be little point in trying to identify herds which had not had a case of BSE since this would apply to almost all the national herds. There is a risk, furthermore, that such a programme in Member Stats other than the UK would be counterproductive, by suggesting that beef from herds not in the scheme is unsafe, which is untrue.

Question No 81 by Mark Killilea (H−0681/96)

Subject: Commission's report on seals and sea mammals

When will the Commission's report on seals and sea mammals, on which research commenced two years ago, be publicly available, so that we can be in a position to assess the total quantities of white fish now being consumed by seals and such mammals, and in order that action may be taken to preserve these scarce stocks of fish through the implementation of humane methods of control?

Answer

The Commission would inform the honourable Member that it is not preparing a document on seals and marine mammals. A study on the subject is being carried out by an expert from Ireland, with financial support from the Commission (study contract No 95/40) within the standard Commission framework of funding. The report on the completed study will not necessarily reflect the views of the Commission and will in no way anticipate the Commission's future policy.

Question No 82 by Elly Plooij‐van Gorsel (H−0685/96)

Subject: Tariff classification of computer equipment

Is it true that the EU has changed the tariff classification of computer equipment, for example LAN equipment, CD‐ROM drives, multimedia PCs and PCTVs, so that, instead of the low tariffs of the Harmonized System, the high tariffs are now applied?

If so, why has the change been made?

Is this reclassification of computer equipment compatible with the tariff obligations arising from the Uruguay Round?

As a result of the increase in tariffs applied to IT products on the European market, are European companies that are internationally active at a competitive disadvantage by comparison with their American and Asian competitors who profit from low tariffs on their markets?

If so, does this constitute a threat for the further development of the European information society and for job creation?

Answer

The Community continues to classify products, including computers and computer hardware, in conformity with the Harmonised System (HS) rules and guidelines established by the World Customs Organisation (WCO). However, given the rapid development of technology in this field, and the multiplication of ‚hybrid’ products there are, from time to time, differences in interpretation of such rules and guidelines by Member States in classifying products. To ensure consistency in interpreting the HS, i.e. finding one heading in the nomenclature of the CCT for a given product, the Community issues regulations and establishes the heading under which a specific product is classified. These matters are dealt with on their own merits (i.e. separate from the economic issue). Where such common classification lead to an undesirable tariff effect, the Commission may propose a tariff reduction or suspension. This was the case, for example, following the common classification of CD‐ROMs.

As a general rule, users of imported products upon which there is a tariff, pay higher prices than they would in its absence. This, in turn, can have an impact on the competitive position of the industry vis‐à‐vis the industry of a trading partner with no tariff. On the other hand, the maintenance of tariffs not only affords a measure of protection to the existing domestic industry but can also help encourage inward investment. Such tariffs affect all industries, not just those in the IT sector.

Where there is no domestic production of a specific product, the import duty may be suspended. In the Community, the IT sector benefits more from such duty suspensions than any other sector thus eliminating or minimising any negative consequences of the IT tariffs referred to in the question.

Given the particular problems associated with the classification of multimedia products, the Commission is currently undertaking an internal review to ensure classification consistency and to examine the tariff impact on the competitivity of IT industry in the Community.

***

Question No 83 by Heidi Hautala (H−0687/96)

Subject: Tax on imported electricity in Finland

In Finland, an energy and carbon dioxide tax was introduced on environmental grounds in 1995. 75 % of it is based on the carbon content of the fuel used and 25 % on its energy content, so that electricity produced using coal condensate is taxed at a rate of 4.3 Finnish pennies per kWh. In order to ensure that the tax did not discriminate against electricity produced in Finland, a tax of 2.2 pennies per kWh was imposed on imported electricity, corresponding to the average tax rate applicable to domestically produced electricity.

It has been alleged that the tax on imported electricity is contrary to EU competition law. How will the Commission make it possible to introduce energy and carbon dioxide taxes in Member States on environmental grounds, if it is not permitted to tax imports in order to prevent discrimination against domestically produced electricity in comparison with imported electricity?

Answer

First of all, the Commission would like to offer the honourable Member certain clarifications regarding the tax on electricity imported into Finland.

In a letter dated 23 November 1995, the Commission apprised the Finnish authorities of the fact that certain aspects of their electricity taxation system did not comply with the principle of fiscal non‐discrimination laid down in Article 95 of the EC Treaty. In particular, the rate at which tax is charged on imported electricity is higher than that charged on some of the electricity produced in Finland. Furthermore, it is at the stage of electricity production, and not consumption, that the CO2 emissions relating thereto are produced, while objective factors do not make it possible to distinguish electricity as a product in accordance with its method of production.

In its answer of 15 April 1996, the Finnish government stated that, in view of these disadvantages, it had decided to carry out a radical reform of the energy tax system no later than 1 January 1997.

The Commission is currently considering a new proposal on the taxation of energy products, to be presented before the end of the year.

Question No 84 by Brian Crowley (H−0689/96)

Subject: Establishing a Community initiative on unemployment blackspots

In the context of the Territorial Employment Pacts (TEPs) which have emerged as an element of the Santer employment pact, would the Commission now consider putting forward a proposal for a Community initiative on Unemployment Blackspots, which would particularly take long‐term unemployment into account?

Answer

The European Council meeting in Florence last June gave its support to the Commission's proposals to the effect that the activities of the structural funds should be more closely focused on the promotion of employment.

On that occasion, the European Council also invited each Member State to select regions or cities suitable for participating in pilot projects relating to regional and local pacts, the purpose of which would be to encourage job creation, especially through the development of local development and employment initiatives.

It is now a matter for each Member State which wishes to do so to submit projects which could be financed, in part, by means of the margins of manoeuvre available within the framework of the current planning of structural policies.

So far as it can, the Commission is prepared to supply such technical and financial assistance as may prove necessary during the preparatory stage of these pacts, after which the measures eligible for the structural funds included in the pacts will have to be presented to the responsible authorities, and especially to the competent monitoring committees.

Question No 85 by Pat Gallagher (H−0692/96)

Subject: Installation of additional facilities on fishing vessels

The installation of additional facilities for safety purposes on fishing vessels can lead to an increase in the vessel's overall tonnage. Will the Commission examine the possibility of exempting this safety tonnage from the calculation of the overall tonnage of vessels in fishing fleets?

Answer

As far as the Commission is concerned, the contradiction between ‚an improvement in safety and hygiene which may result in an increase in a vessel's tonnage’ and ‚compliance with the global objectives of the multiannual guidance programmes (MAGPs)’ only exists in so far as certain Member States have not taken the necessary steps to promote this modernization while complying with the objectives of fleet management.

Consequently, knowing that the Commission has always defended – and will continue to defend – the improving of shipboard life and working conditions for the Community's fishermen, the Commission favours clarification of the real needs as they arise.

Since no one can dispute that any increase in the tonnage or individual power of a vessel, motivated by safety reasons, will result in additional fishing performance, the Commission, in the interests of guaranteeing the necessary transparency, would be favourable to any national initiative which might help to improve safety without, as a consequence, obstructing the objectives of the MAGPs, especially by the establishment of a specific modernization plan.

Question No 86 by Ulla Sandbæk (H−0695/96)

Subject: Additives and organic farming

The Commission is asked to report on the negotiations and any proposals discussed in the Codex Alimentarius Committee for Food Labelling concerning the use of eco‐labels. In this connection, will it guarantee that there will be no easing of restrictions, as a result of initiatives from Codex Alimentarius or from the Commission itself, in the form of allowing greater use of additives and pesticides when compared with the EU's rules for organic farming?

Answer

The draft guidelines for the production, labelling and marketing of organically produced foods being developed by the Codex Committee on Food Labelling are presently at stage 6 of the 8 stages of the Codex procedure. The Commission and Member States are participating actively and presented a common position at the Committee's last meeting in Ottawa in May 1996. In relation to products of plant origin, the list of authorised pesticides and additives is almost the same as those authorized in the Community under Regulation (EEC) No 2092/91 and the Community is seeking to tighten the provisions of the guidelines establishing the criteria for authorizing new products in the future.

With regard to products of animal origin, for which the Commission submitted a proposal to Council on 25 July 1996, the Commission feels it would be premature for Codex at this stage to establish a similar list of authorized additives.

Subject: Secession from the Italian State

Mr Bossi, self‐styled chief of the Celtic tribes that entered Northern Italy between 500 and 400 BC, told the Italian newspaper La Stampa on 4 September 1996 that the European Union, in turning down the request by the imaginary ‚Padania’ to embark on European Monetary Union with its own currency, actually wished to encourage the transformation of the ‚Region of Padania’ – which, incidentally, does not exist – into a free State.

Is Mr Bossi's interpretation of the decision by the European Union accurate‐ in which case the European Union has interfered arbitrarily in Italy's internal affairs ‐or is this another of Mr Bossi's habitual free interpretations?

Answer

First of all, the Commission would like to remind the honourable Member that it is not in the habit of commenting on press reports.

However, as far as this specific question is concerned, the Commission notes that no European Union decision exists on the subject mentioned, but that a Commission spokesman answered questions put by journalists regarding the statements by Mr Bossi published in the Italian press.

These replies, without going into detail, are confined to restating the provisions of the Community treaties on the subject of participation in economic and monetary union.

Question No 88 by Doeke Eisma (H−0698/96)

Subject: World Solar Summit in Harare on 16 and 17 September 1996

The World Solar Summit took place in Harare (Zimbabwe) on 16 and 17 September 1996, with both the Member States of the European Union and the Commission taking part.

What was the Commission's participation in this Conference and what was its action there?

Does the Commission intend to inform Parliament of the results of the conference?

Does it intend to make new proposals to encourage the use of sustainable energy in the Third World and also in the European Union?

Answer

The World Solar Summit which takes place in Harare, Zimbabwe, on 16 and 17 September 1996 is the culminating point of a process guided by UNESCO which effectively began in 1993.

A number of regional meetings have been held over the last 3 years to prepare for the Summit of Heads of State and Government in Harare, where it is expected that a political declaration will be made on the promotion of renewable energy sources at world level and that an outline World Solar Programme will be presented, comprising priority projects to be implemented in the ten year period 1996‐2005.

The European Commission has participated in and financially supported regional activities in preparation for the Harare Summit, with respect both to the Mediterranean region and to the CIS and the Central and Eastern European States. It also plans to support the World Solar Summit as such in Harare, where it will be represented by the Commissioner responsible for energy.

The results of this Summit will be widely distributed and brought to the attention of Parliament.

Furthermore, the Commission will, during the coming months, be presenting to the Council and to Parliament a global strategy for promoting the use of renewable energy sources in the Community. As far as third countries are concerned, a stronger emphasis should be placed on renewable energy sources within the available programmes, such as the new MEDIA programme for the Mediterranean region.

Question No 89 by Friedrich Wolf (H−0710/96)

Subject: Effectiveness of subsidies to large firms in creating employment

Is the Commission in a position to assess, in terms of numbers of jobs, the effectiveness, in the medium‐term, of paying employment policy subsidies to large undertakings prepared to set up in crisis regions? Can it estimate the effectiveness of the same level of funding if it were put into local employment initiatives? In particular, is the Commission able to assess the viability of the concept of a ‚pool of sponsorship’ intended to link the promotion of big business with local and regional economic development?

Answer

When it examines employment aid in the light of the rules governing competition, the Commission verifies that such aid is associated with the creation of new jobs in small and medium‐sized enterprises or in regions which are eligible for aid for regional purposes, and that it does not exceed the level necessary in order to achieve the intended objectives. It also authorizes this type of aid when it is intended to encourage enterprises to recruit certain categories of workers who experience particular difficulties in becoming integrated or reintegrated into the labour market.

It is generally admitted that the effectiveness of employment aid cannot be measured directly in terms of the number of jobs subsidized in this way. This is because of the substitution or windfall effects associated with the measures in question, which are well known but difficult to quantify. Furthermore, the effects in terms of employment of the aid granted by the Commission in order to attract investment into the least developed regions are staggered over the course of time and affect both the undertaking that benefits from the aid directly and the other economic activities triggered by the new investment (especially supplier activities and commercial activities). Finally, the hoped‐for effects may be influenced by other factors which are difficult to isolate in a quantitative analysis.

The role of the Commission is to ensure that the Member States do not implement aid programmes which might result in distortions of competition that are disproportionate in terms of the objectives pursued. Furthermore, the Commission is responsible for the ongoing review of systems implemented after its approval, a task which it undertakes primarily through individual analysis of the annual reports sent to it by the Member States.

***

Question No 90 by José Valverde López (H−0712/96)

Subject: Fraud in Andalusia concerning sales of olive oil

There have been press reports of fraud concerning the bottling and sale of olive oil extensively adulterated with other vegetable oils in Andalusia (Spain).

Since the manufacture, storage and bottling of olive oil, and promotion of its consumption, are funded by the EU budget, what steps has the Commission taken to ascertain the scale of fraud in this case, and what penalties are likely to be imposed?

Answer

The Commission became aware of a suspicious case of fraud concerning olive oil in Spain (Andalucia) in June 1996. It asked for information from the Spanish olive oil inspection agency (AAO) which belongs to the Ministry of Agriculture. The agency replied that it was carrying out investigations.

As a direct result of these inquiries, criminal proceedings are now under way in Spain involving two companies. The importance of the fraud case will be checked in this context. Sanctions could be taken later on at the national level in compliance with Community law (particularly as regards penalties and withdrawal of agreement according to the provisions of regulation (EEC) No 2677/85(17) ). The Commission will follow these procedures with great attention.

The Commission would moreover point out that the task group created at European level in the olive oil sector, as indicated in its 1995 and 1996 anti‐fraud work programmes(18) , became operational early in 1996. This task group intends to centralize sensitive information in order to react appropriately with coordination of inquiries and, where necessary, the participation of Commission officials.

Question No 91 by Jaime Valdivielso de Cué (H−0715/96)

Subject: Territorial pact for employment

The Commission's plans to create a European strategy to stimulate local development and employment initiatives is to be welcomed, as is President Santer's initiative regarding funding of pilot schemes such as the ‚territorial pact for employment’. Will the Commission, when examining the application by the Basque Country, take into consideration the high rate of youth unemployment, with its effects in terms of crime and violence, currently affecting the region? Will it, in particular, consider introducing a genuine ‚pact’ bringing together all sectors of society? Question No 92 by Anne McIntosh (H−0514/96)

Subject: EU enforcement of the Common Fisheries Policy

What measures does the Commission propose to take, in addition to those already taken, to ensure that all Member States abide equally by the terms of the Common Fisheries Policy?

Is it aware that certain Member States are firmly implementing the Common Fisheries Policy, whereas others are wilfully lax in their application of the necessary controls?

What steps does it propose to take to ensure strict enforcement of the quotas set?

Answer

Within the framework of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) enforcement falls within the exclusive competence of the Member States(19) . It is the task of the Commission to ensure that Member States fulfil their control obligations in a non‐discriminatory manner. To carry out this task more effectively, the Commission is taking measures which aim at increasing transparency between the control services of individual Member States. In that context, the deployment of new technologies, such as satellites and computerized networks, can be considered to be a milestone in the field of control. Another decisive step towards greater transparency is the Annual Report on ‚Monitoring the Common Fisheries Policy’(20) , which focuses on the strengths and weaknesses in the national enforcement of the CFP. This document highlights the need to bring about major improvements with respect to catch validation, application of technical measures, etc. In addition, the Commission is committed to putting more pressure on those Member States which fail to comply with the rules of the CFP and to applying, if necessary, the procedures provided for in the Treaty so as to ensure that every single Member State discharges its control obligations in full.

The Commission is fully aware of the differences in the level of enforcement in individual Member States. However, to suggest that some Member States discharge their control obligations in full while other are ‚wilfully lax’ distorts what is a very complex situation and is not justified.

With respect to the monitoring of national quotas, it is expected that major improvements can be achieved thorough the implementation of anew Council Regulation(21) adopted recently which allows for greater flexibility in the year‐to‐year management of TACs and quotas and which authorizes the Commission to operate deductions from quotas when overfishing has taken place. In addition, major benefits can be derived from the establishment of computerized data bases ad computer networks to be introduced in 1996 in the Member States and which allow for easy cross‐checking of data.

Question No 93 by Luciano Vecchi (H−0528/96)

Subject: EU Delegation for Somalia

The EU Delegation for Somalia, which is located in Nairobi (Kenya), has played an essential role in the provision of EU aid to Somalia and as a point of reference for the action of international donors, e.g. via the creation of the SCB (Somalia Aid Coordination Body).

Can the Commission state whether there is in any truth in the rumours that it intends to close the delegation for Somalia in Nairobi? If so, can it explain why, in view of the dramatic potential consequences of any such decision?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the excellent results achieved by the Somalia Unit and is committed to remain actively engaged in Somalia. It does not intend to close down the Commission work in Nairobi, but administrative arrangements may be reviewed.

Question No 94 by Arthur Newens (H−0543/96)

Subject: Press freedom of expression

In response to my question to the Council (H−0404/96)(22) about problems being faced by publications with limited circulation and which are increasingly not being handled by the large distribution companies, the President‐in‐Office expressed how much importance the Council attaches to press freedom and plurality of expression. However, he pointed out that the responsibility for overseeing the media lies with the Commission under Article 155. He went on to say that he would welcome proposals from the Commission to deal with this issue. Would the Commissioner therefore please comment and let me know if this issue is currently under investigation and what can be done to prevent discrimination against such publications?

Answer

The Commission has powers, under the EC competition rules, to examine restrictive agreements or practices resulting from an abuse of a dominant position. However, no specific complaint has been received by the Commission from editors of small circulation publications within the Community that their commercial existence is under threat as a result of anti‐competitive behaviour by large distribution companies. For this reason, the issue referred to by the honourable Member is not currently under investigation under Article 85 or Article 86 of the EC Treaty.

The Commission is currently working on a possible initiative concerning the harmonization of specific national media ownership rules aiming to safeguard pluralism. However, this work does not focus on the question of the behaviour of economic operators in the newspaper distribution market because the current national rules on media ownership do not cover these kind of questions.

Question No 95 by Edgar Schiedermeier (H−0547/96)

Subject: Local employment initiatives

What progress has been made with the local employment initiatives in the European Union in 1996?

Are there encouraging projects in the Federal Republic of Germany?

Answer

The promotion of local development is part of the European Employment Strategy as defined and agreed by the European Council in Essen, and reinforced by subsequent European Councils. In particular, they have underlined the importance of the development of local employment initiatives in the fields of services linked with the environment and living standards, crafts and traditional products. Since the Commission adopted the Communication ‚A European Strategy for Encouraging Local Development and Employment Initiatives’ in June 1995, Community institutions have confirmed the importance of support to the local development approach.

The European Council in Florence this year also supported the approach proposed by the Commission's Communication ‚Action for Employment in Europe – A Confidence Pact’ of an open and flexible process, enabling all those concerned to enter specific commitments at their level of responsibility. This approach will give new impetus to local development, in particular in the framework of territorial and local employment pacts. The Commission will be involved in the development, selection and implementation of these pacts and the contribution which the Structural Funds can make will also be examined. A further positive signal is expected from the Dublin Conference in November which the Irish presidency will devote to local employment initiatives.

As far as innovative actions are concerned, Article 6 of the European Social Fund provides the scope for local employment initiatives. In 1996, some 25 MECUS will be spent on actions promoting new sources of jobs.

Within the framework of regional policy, through Article 10 of the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), 41 innovative projects of new sources of jobs will be launched, in areas such as care and domestic services, the improvement of living conditions and of the environment. Five of these will be in Germany.

The Community Initiatives Employment and ADAPT have of course an important role to play in the context of local development, through a varied series of actions in all Member States. In Germany, where the ESF budget for the two Initiatives represents some 455 MECU, almost 500 projects have been selected in the first of two phases. As an example, one of these projects, based in Triers, aims to link the development of tourism in the city and employment prospects of young migrant people with low qualifications, through on‐the‐job training.

In rural areas, the rural villages development actions are of outstanding importance to promote local development, stabilize the services, widen the economic activities to improve the quality of life in order to attract other activities to these rural settlements. In addition, the LEADER II Initiative – ’Links between Actions for the Development of the Rural Economy’ favours local development strategies mainly based on local initiative groups in order to test innovative strategies under practical circumstances.

There are a number of examples of good practice in Germany involving job creation projects for the long‐term unemployed and welfare beneficiaries. In Shweinfurt in Bavaria for example former industrial workers are given training and work‐experience in the areas of environmental protection, social work and youth care.

Another project in Sachsen, one of the ‚New Länder’, aims to reduce the prospects of unemployment among women employed in the textile industry whilst at the same time increasing competitivity, through a programme of on‐the‐job training. These projects, and there are many others, are often ambitious co‐financing exercises, involving funds from the employment agency, Land and federal governments, and the ESF.

The Commission attaches great importance to the promotion of local development initiatives and the issues which they address. A wealth of information is at our disposal in the form of research and evaluation, some of which have already been made available to the honourable Member.
